<h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">Arabic</h1>
<div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"><span class="mw-redirectedfrom">  (Redirected from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/w/index.php?title=Arabic_language&amp;redirect=no" title="Arabic language">Arabic language</a>)</span></div>
<div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
</div>
<div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en"><div class="mw-parser-output"><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">This article is about the general language or language group. For the literary standard, see <a href="/wiki/Modern_Standard_Arabic" title="Modern Standard Arabic">Modern Standard Arabic</a>. For the various vernaculars, see <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Arabic" title="Varieties of Arabic">varieties of Arabic</a>. For the language family, see <a href="/wiki/Arabic_languages" title="Arabic languages">Arabic languages</a>.</div>

<p><b>Arabic</b> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar">العَرَبِيَّة</span>‎‎, <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Arabic transliteration">al-ʻarabiyyah</span></i> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Arabic" title="Help:IPA/Arabic">[ʔalʕaraˈbijːah]</a></span><small class="nowrap metadata"> (<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:Al_arabic.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a> </span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/8/8e/Al_arabic.ogg" title="Al arabic.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">Arabic</a>: <span dir="rtl" lang="ar">عَرَبِيّ</span>‎‎ <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="ar ALA transliteration">ʻarabī</span></i> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Arabic" title="Help:IPA/Arabic">[ˈʕarabiː]</a></span><small class="nowrap metadata"> (<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap"><a href="/wiki/File:Arabi.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a> </span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c4/Arabi.ogg" title="Arabi.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</small> or <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Arabic" title="Help:IPA/Arabic">[ʕaraˈbijː]</a></span>) is a <a href="/wiki/Central_Semitic_languages" title="Central Semitic languages">Central Semitic</a> language complex that first emerged in <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> northwestern <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabia</a> and is now the <i><a href="/wiki/Lingua_franca" title="Lingua franca">lingua franca</a></i> of the <a href="/wiki/Arab_world" title="Arab world">Arab world</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> It is named after the <a href="/wiki/Arabs" title="Arabs">Arabs</a>, a term initially used to describe peoples living from <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamia" title="Mesopotamia">Mesopotamia</a> in the east to the <a href="/wiki/Anti-Lebanon_Mountains" title="Anti-Lebanon Mountains">Anti-Lebanon mountains</a> in the west, in northwestern Arabia, and in the <a href="/wiki/Sinai_Peninsula" title="Sinai Peninsula">Sinai peninsula</a>.</p>
<p>The modern written language (<a href="/wiki/Modern_Standard_Arabic" title="Modern Standard Arabic">Modern Standard Arabic</a>) is derived from <a href="/wiki/Classical_Arabic" title="Classical Arabic">Classical Arabic</a>. It is widely taught in schools and universities, and is used to varying degrees in workplaces, government, and the media. The two formal varieties are grouped together as Literary Arabic (<i>fuṣḥā</i>), which is the official language of 26 states and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Liturgical_language" title="Liturgical language">liturgical language</a> of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>. Modern Standard Arabic largely follows the grammatical standards of Classical Arabic and uses much of the same <a href="/wiki/Vocabulary" title="Vocabulary">vocabulary</a>. However, it has discarded some grammatical constructions and vocabulary that no longer have any counterpart in the spoken varieties, and has adopted certain new constructions and vocabulary from the spoken varieties. Much of the new vocabulary is used to denote concepts that have arisen in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Post-classical_era" title="Post-classical era">post-classical era</a>, especially in modern times.</p>
<p>During the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, Literary Arabic was a major vehicle of culture in Europe, especially in science, mathematics and philosophy. As a result, many European languages have also <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">borrowed</a> many words from it. Arabic influence, mainly in vocabulary, is seen in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/European_languages" title="European languages">European languages</a>, mainly <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic_influence_on_the_Spanish_language" title="Arabic influence on the Spanish language">Spanish</a> and to a lesser extent Portuguese, Valencian and Catalan, owing to both the proximity of Christian European and Muslim Arab civilizations and 800 years of Arabic culture and language in the <a href="/wiki/Iberian_Peninsula" title="Iberian Peninsula">Iberian Peninsula</a>, referred to in Arabic as <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">al-Andalus</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sicilian_dialect" title="Sicilian dialect">Sicilian</a> has about 500 Arabic words as result of Sicily being progressively conquered by Saracens from North Africa, from the mid 9th to mid 10th centuries. Many of these words relate to agriculture and related activities (Hull and Ruffino). <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balkan" title="Balkan">Balkan</a> languages, including <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bulgarian_language" title="Bulgarian language">Bulgarian</a>, have also acquired a significant number of Arabic words through contact with <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Turkish_language" title="Ottoman Turkish language">Ottoman Turkish</a>.</p>
<p>Arabic has influenced many languages around the globe throughout its history. Some of the most influenced languages are <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maltese_language" title="Maltese language">Maltese</a><b>,</b> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urdu_language" title="Urdu language">Urdu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kashmiri_language" title="Kashmiri language">Kashmiri</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kurdish_language" title="Kurdish language">Kurdish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bosnian_language" title="Bosnian language">Bosnian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kazakh_language" title="Kazakh language">Kazakh</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bengali_language" title="Bengali language">Bengali</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hindi" title="Hindi">Hindi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malay_language" title="Malay language">Malay</a>, <a href="/wiki/Maldivian_language" title="Maldivian language">Maldivian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indonesian_language" title="Indonesian language">Indonesian</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pashto_language" title="Pashto language">Pashto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Punjabi_language" title="Punjabi language">Punjabi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tagalog_language" title="Tagalog language">Tagalog</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sindhi_language" title="Sindhi language">Sindhi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hausa_language" title="Hausa language">Hausa</a> and some languages in parts of Africa. Conversely, Arabic has borrowed words from other languages including Greek and Persian in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Medieval" title="Medieval">medieval</a> times, and contemporary European languages such as <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a> and <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a> in modern times.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Classical_Arabic" title="Classical Arabic">Classical Arabic</a> is the <a href="/wiki/Sacred_language" title="Sacred language">liturgical language</a> of 1.7 billion <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslims" title="Muslims">Muslims</a> and <a href="/wiki/Modern_Standard_Arabic" title="Modern Standard Arabic">Modern Standard Arabic</a> is one of six <a href="/wiki/Official_languages_of_the_United_Nations" title="Official languages of the United Nations">official languages of the United Nations</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-gordonconwell.edu_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-gordonconwell.edu-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pewmuslim4_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-pewmuslim4-7">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> It is spoken by perhaps as many as 422 million speakers (native and non-native) in the Arab world,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup> making it the fifth most spoken language in the world. Arabic is written with the Arabic alphabet, which is an <a href="/wiki/Abjad" title="Abjad">abjad</a> script and is written from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Right_to_left" title="Right to left">right to left</a>, although the spoken varieties are sometimes <a href="/wiki/Arabic_chat_alphabet" title="Arabic chat alphabet">written in ASCII Latin</a> from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Left_to_right" title="Left to right">left to right</a> with no standardized orthography.</p>
<p></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Arabic is a <a href="/wiki/Central_Semitic_languages" title="Central Semitic languages">Central Semitic</a> language, closely related to the <a href="/wiki/Northwest_Semitic_languages" title="Northwest Semitic languages">Northwest Semitic languages</a> (<a href="/wiki/Aramaic_language" title="Aramaic language">Aramaic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_language" title="Hebrew language">Hebrew</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ugaritic_language" title="Ugaritic language">Ugaritic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Phoenician_language" title="Phoenician language">Phoenician</a>), the Ancient South Arabian languages, and various other Semitic languages of Arabia such as <a href="/wiki/Dadanitic" title="Dadanitic">Dadanitic</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Semitic_languages" title="Semitic languages">Semitic languages</a> changed a great deal between <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Semitic" title="Proto-Semitic">Proto-Semitic</a> and the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/Central_Semitic_languages" title="Central Semitic languages">Central Semitic languages</a>, particularly in grammar. Innovations of the Central Semitic languages—all maintained in Arabic—include:</p>
<ol>
<li>The conversion of the suffix-conjugated stative formation (<i>jalas-</i>) into a past tense.</li>
<li>The conversion of the prefix-conjugated preterite-tense formation (<i>yajlis-</i>) into a present tense.</li>
<li>The elimination of other prefix-conjugated mood/aspect forms (e.g., a present tense formed by doubling the middle root, a perfect formed by infixing a <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/t/</span> after the first root consonant, probably a jussive formed by a stress shift) in favor of new moods formed by endings attached to the prefix-conjugation forms (e.g., <i>-u</i> for indicative, <i>-a</i> for subjunctive, no ending for jussive, <i>-an</i> or <i>-anna</i> for energetic).</li>
<li>The development of an internal passive.</li>
</ol>
<p>There are several features which Classical Arabic, the modern Arabic varieties, as well as the <a href="/wiki/Safaitic" title="Safaitic">Safaitic</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hismaic_(Old_Arabic)" title="Hismaic (Old Arabic)">Hismaic</a> inscriptions share which are unattested in any other Central Semitic language variety, including the <a href="/wiki/Dadanitic" title="Dadanitic">Dadanitic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taymanitic" title="Taymanitic">Taymanitic</a> languages of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hijaz" title="Hijaz">northern Hijaz</a>. These features are evidence of common descent from a <a href="/wiki/Proto-language" title="Proto-language">hypothetical ancestor</a>, <a href="/wiki/Proto-Arabic" title="Proto-Arabic">Proto-Arabic</a>. The following features can be reconstructed with confidence for Proto-Arabic:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></p>
<ol>
<li>negative particles <i>m</i> *<i>mā</i>; <i>lʾn</i> *<i>lā-ʾan</i> &gt; CAr <i>lan</i></li>
<li><i>mafʿūl</i> G-passive participle</li>
<li>prepositions and adverbs <i>f</i>, <i>ʿn</i>, <i>ʿnd</i>, <i>ḥt</i>, <i>ʿkdy</i></li>
<li>a subjunctive in -<i>a</i></li>
<li><i>t</i>-demonstratives</li>
<li>leveling of the -<i>at</i> allomorph of the feminine ending</li>
<li><i>ʾn</i> complementizer and subordinator</li>
<li>the use of <i>f</i>- to introduce modal clauses</li>
<li>independent object pronoun in (<i>ʾ</i>)<i>y</i></li>
<li>vestiges of <i>nunation</i></li>
</ol>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Old_Arabic">Old Arabic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Old Arabic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Old_Arabic" title="Old Arabic">Old Arabic</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arabian_Languages.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="507" data-file-width="571" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Arabian_Languages.png/220px-Arabian_Languages.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Arabian_Languages.png/330px-Arabian_Languages.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0e/Arabian_Languages.png/440px-Arabian_Languages.png 2x" width="220"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arabian_Languages.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Arabian Languages</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Arabia boasted a wide variety of Semitic languages in antiquity. In the southwest, various <a href="/wiki/Central_Semitic_languages" title="Central Semitic languages">Central Semitic languages</a> both belonging to and outside of the <a href="/wiki/Old_South_Arabian" title="Old South Arabian">Ancient South Arabian</a> family (e.g. Southern Thamudic) were spoken. It is also believed that the ancestors of the <a href="/wiki/Modern_South_Arabian_languages" title="Modern South Arabian languages">Modern South Arabian languages</a> (non-Central Semitic languages) were also spoken in southern Arabia at this time. To the north, in the oases of northern <a href="/wiki/Hejaz" title="Hejaz">Hijaz</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dadanitic" title="Dadanitic">Dadanitic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taymanitic" title="Taymanitic">Taymanitic</a> held some prestige as inscriptional languages. In <a href="/wiki/Najd" title="Najd">Najd</a> and parts of western Arabia, a language known to scholars as Thamudic C is attested. In eastern Arabia, inscriptions in a script derived from ASA attest to a language known as <a href="/wiki/Hasaitic_dialect" title="Hasaitic dialect">Hasaitic</a>. Finally, on the northwestern frontier of Arabia, various languages known to scholars as Thamudic B, Thamudic D, <a href="/wiki/Safaitic" title="Safaitic">Safaitic</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hismaic" title="Hismaic">Hismaic</a> are attested. The last two share important isoglosses with later forms of Arabic, leading scholars to theorize that Safaitic and Hismaic are in fact early forms of Arabic and that they should be considered <a href="/wiki/Old_Arabic" title="Old Arabic">Old Arabic</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></p>
<p>Beginning in the 1st century CE, fragments of Northern Old Arabic are attested in the <a href="/wiki/Nabataean_alphabet" title="Nabataean alphabet">Nabataean script</a> across northern Arabia. By the 4th century CE, the Nabataean Aramaic writing system had come to express varieties of Arabic other than that of the Nabataeans.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Old_Higazi_and_Classical_Arabic">Old Higazi and Classical Arabic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Old Higazi and Classical Arabic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In late pre-Islamic times, a transdialectal and transcommunal variety of Arabic emerged in the <a href="/wiki/Hejaz" title="Hejaz">Hijaz</a> which continued living its parallel life after literary Arabic had been institutionally standardized in the 2nd and 3rd century of the <a href="/wiki/Islamic_calendar" title="Islamic calendar">Hijra</a>, most strongly in Judeo-Christian texts, keeping alive ancient features eliminated from the "learned" tradition (Classical Arabic).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-13">[13]</a></sup> This variety and both its classicizing and "lay" iterations have been termed Middle Arabic in the past, but they are thought to continue an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Old_Higazi" title="Old Higazi">Old Higazi</a> register. It is clear that the orthography of the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Qur'ān</a> was not developed for the standardized form of Classical Arabic; rather, it shows the attempt on the part of writers to record an archaic form of Old Higazi.</p>
<p>In the late 6th century AD, a relatively uniform intertribal "poetic koine" distinct from the <a href="/wiki/Vernacular" title="Vernacular">spoken vernaculars</a> developed based on the <a href="/wiki/Bedouin" title="Bedouin">Bedouin</a> dialects of <a href="/wiki/Najd" title="Najd">Najd</a>, probably in connection with the court of <a href="/wiki/Al-Hirah" title="Al-Hirah">al-Ḥīra</a>. During the first Islamic century, the majority of Arabic poets and Arabic-writing persons spoke Arabic as their mother tongue. Their texts, although mainly preserved in far later manuscripts, contain traces of non-standardized <a href="/wiki/Classical_Arabic" title="Classical Arabic">Classical Arabic</a> elements in morphology and syntax. The standardization of Classical Arabic reached completion around the end of the 8th century. The first comprehensive description of the <i>ʿarabiyya</i> "Arabic", <a href="/wiki/Sibawayh" title="Sibawayh">Sībawayhi's</a> <i>al</i>-<i>Kitāb</i>, is based first of all upon a corpus of poetic texts, in addition to Qur'ān usage and Bedouin informants whom he considered to be reliable speakers of the <i>ʿarabiyya</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-referenceworks.brillonline.com_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-referenceworks.brillonline.com-14">[14]</a></sup> By the 8th century, knowledge of Classical Arabic had become an essential prerequisite for rising into the higher classes throughout the Islamic world.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Neo-Arabic">Neo-Arabic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Neo-Arabic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Charles Ferguson's <a href="/wiki/Koin%C3%A9_language" title="Koiné language">koine</a> theory (Ferguson 1959) claims that the modern Arabic dialects collectively descend from a single military koine that sprang up during the Islamic conquests; this view has been challenged in recent times. Ahmad al-Jallad proposes that there were at least two considerably distinct types of Arabic on the eve of the conquests: Northern and Central (Al-Jallad 2009). The modern dialects emerged from a new contact situation produced following the conquests. Instead of the emergence of a single or multiple koines, the dialects contain several sedimentary layers of borrowed and areal features, which they absorbed at different points in their linguistic histories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-referenceworks.brillonline.com_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-referenceworks.brillonline.com-14">[14]</a></sup> According to Veersteegh and Bickerton, colloquial Arabic dialects arose from pidginized Arabic formed from contact between Arabs and conquered peoples. Pidginization and subsequent creolization among Arabs and <a href="/wiki/Arabization" title="Arabization">arabized</a> peoples could explain relative morphological and phonological simplicity of vernacular Arabic compared to Classical and MSA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span id="Classical,_Modern_Standard_and_spoken_Arabic"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical.2C_Modern_Standard_and_spoken_Arabic">Classical, Modern Standard and spoken Arabic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Classical, Modern Standard and spoken Arabic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Arabic_dictionaries" title="List of Arabic dictionaries">List of Arabic dictionaries</a></div>
<p><i>Arabic</i> usually designates one of three main variants: <a href="/wiki/Classical_Arabic" title="Classical Arabic">Classical Arabic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Modern_Standard_Arabic" title="Modern Standard Arabic">Modern Standard Arabic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Arabic" title="Varieties of Arabic"><i>colloquial</i> or <i>dialectal</i> Arabic</a>. Classical Arabic is the language found in the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a>, used from the period of <a href="/wiki/Pre-Islamic_Arabia" title="Pre-Islamic Arabia">Pre-Islamic Arabia</a> to that of the <a href="/wiki/Abbasid_Caliphate" title="Abbasid Caliphate">Abbasid Caliphate</a>. Theoretically, Classical Arabic is considered normative, according to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syntactic" title="Syntactic">syntactic</a> and grammatical norms laid down by classical grammarians (such as <a href="/wiki/Sibawayh" title="Sibawayh">Sibawayh</a>) and the vocabulary defined in classical dictionaries (such as the <a href="/wiki/Ibn_Manzur#Lis.C4.81n_al-.CA.BFArab" title="Ibn Manzur">Lisān al-ʻArab</a>). In practice, however, modern authors almost never write in pure Classical Arabic, instead using a <a href="/wiki/Literary_language" title="Literary language">literary language</a> with its own grammatical norms and vocabulary, commonly known as Modern Standard Arabic (MSA).</p>
<p>MSA is the variety used in most current, printed Arabic publications, spoken by some of the <a href="/wiki/Arabic_media" title="Arabic media">Arabic media</a> across <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Middle_East" title="Middle East">Middle East</a>, and understood by most educated Arabic speakers. "Literary Arabic" and "Standard Arabic" (<span lang="ar">فُصْحَى</span> <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Arabic transliteration">fuṣḥá</span></i>) are less strictly defined terms that may refer to Modern Standard Arabic or Classical Arabic.</p>
<p>Some of the differences between Classical Arabic (CA) and Modern Standard Arabic (MSA) are as follows:</p>
<ul>
<li>Certain grammatical constructions of CA that have no counterpart in any modern dialect (e.g., the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Energetic_mood" title="Energetic mood">energetic mood</a>) are almost never used in <a href="/wiki/Modern_Standard_Arabic" title="Modern Standard Arabic">Modern Standard Arabic</a>.</li>
<li>No modern spoken variety of Arabic has <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_case" title="Grammatical case">case</a> distinctions. As a result, MSA is generally composed without case distinctions in mind, and the proper cases are added after the fact, when necessary. Because most case endings are noted using final short vowels, which are normally left unwritten in the Arabic script, it is unnecessary to determine the proper case of most words. The practical result of this is that MSA, like English and <a href="/wiki/Standard_Chinese" title="Standard Chinese">Standard Chinese</a>, is written in a strongly determined word order and alternative orders that were used in CA for emphasis are rare. In addition, because of the lack of case marking in the spoken varieties, most speakers cannot consistently use the correct endings in extemporaneous speech. As a result, spoken MSA tends to drop or regularize the endings except when reading from a prepared text.</li>
<li>The numeral system in CA is complex and heavily tied in with the case system. This system is never used in MSA, even in the most formal of circumstances; instead, a significantly simplified system is used, approximating the system of the conservative spoken varieties.</li>
</ul>
<p>MSA uses much Classical vocabulary (e.g., <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="ar ALA transliteration">dhahaba</span></i> 'to go') that is not present in the spoken varieties, but deletes Classical words that sound obsolete in MSA. In addition, MSA has borrowed or coined a large number of terms for concepts that did not exist in Quranic times, and MSA continues to evolve.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> Some words have been borrowed from other languages—notice that transliteration mainly indicates spelling and not real pronunciation (e.g., <span lang="ar">فِلْم</span> <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="ar ALA transliteration">film</span></i> 'film' or <span lang="ar">ديمقراطية</span> <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="ar ALA transliteration">dīmuqrāṭiyyah</span></i> 'democracy').</p>
<p>However, the current preference is to avoid direct borrowings, preferring to either use <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Loan_translation" title="Loan translation">loan translations</a> (e.g., <span lang="ar">فرع</span> <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="ar ALA transliteration">farʻ</span></i> 'branch', also used for the branch of a company or organization; <span lang="ar">جناح</span> <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="ar ALA transliteration">janāḥ</span></i> 'wing', is also used for the wing of an airplane, building, air force, etc.), or to coin new words using forms within existing <a href="/wiki/Semitic_root" title="Semitic root">roots</a> (<span lang="ar">استماتة</span> <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Arabic transliteration">istimātah</span></i> '<a href="/wiki/Apoptosis" title="Apoptosis">apoptosis</a>', using the root <span lang="ar">موت</span> <i>m/w/t</i> 'death' put into the <a href="/wiki/Arabic_verbs#Form_X" title="Arabic verbs">Xth form</a>, or <span lang="ar">جامعة</span> <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="ar ALA transliteration">jāmiʻah</span></i> 'university', based on <span lang="ar">جمع</span> <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="ar ALA transliteration">jamaʻa</span></i> 'to gather, unite'; <span lang="ar">جمهورية</span> <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="ar ALA transliteration">jumhūriyyah</span></i> 'republic', based on <span lang="ar">جمهور</span> <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Arabic transliteration">jumhūr</span></i> 'multitude'). An earlier tendency was to redefine an older word although this has fallen into disuse (e.g., <span lang="ar">هاتف</span> <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Arabic transliteration">hātif</span></i> 'telephone' &lt; 'invisible caller (in Sufism)'; <span lang="ar">جريدة</span> <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Arabic transliteration">jarīdah</span></i> 'newspaper' &lt; 'palm-leaf stalk').</p>
<p><i>Colloquial</i> or <i>dialectal</i> Arabic refers to the many national or regional varieties which constitute the everyday spoken language and evolved from Classical Arabic. Colloquial Arabic has many regional variants; geographically distant varieties usually differ enough to be <a href="/wiki/Mutual_intelligibility" title="Mutual intelligibility">mutually unintelligible</a>, and some linguists consider them distinct languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> The varieties are typically unwritten. They are often used in informal spoken media, such as <a href="/wiki/Soap_opera" title="Soap opera">soap operas</a> and <a href="/wiki/Talk_show" title="Talk show">talk shows</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jenkins_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jenkins-19">[19]</a></sup> as well as occasionally in certain forms of written media such as poetry and printed advertising.</p>
<p>The only variety of modern Arabic to have acquired official language status is <a href="/wiki/Maltese_language" title="Maltese language">Maltese</a>, which is spoken in (predominately <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic</a>) <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">Malta</a> and written with the <a href="/wiki/Maltese_alphabet" title="Maltese alphabet">Latin script</a>. It is descended from Classical Arabic through <a href="/wiki/Siculo-Arabic" title="Siculo-Arabic">Siculo-Arabic</a>, but is not mutually intelligible with any other variety of Arabic. Most linguists list it as a separate language rather than as a dialect of Arabic.</p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_the_Arab_League.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="900" height="113" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Flag_of_the_Arab_League.svg/170px-Flag_of_the_Arab_League.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Flag_of_the_Arab_League.svg/255px-Flag_of_the_Arab_League.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2b/Flag_of_the_Arab_League.svg/340px-Flag_of_the_Arab_League.svg.png 2x" width="170"/></a>

<p>Even during Muhammad's lifetime, there were dialects of spoken Arabic. Muhammad spoke in the dialect of <a href="/wiki/Mecca" title="Mecca">Mecca</a>, in the western <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabian_peninsula" title="Arabian peninsula">Arabian peninsula</a>, and it was in this dialect that the Quran was written down. However, the dialects of the eastern Arabian peninsula were considered the most prestigious at the time, so the language of the Quran was ultimately converted to follow the eastern <a href="/wiki/Phonology" title="Phonology">phonology</a>. It is this phonology that underlies the modern pronunciation of Classical Arabic. The phonological differences between these two dialects account for some of the complexities of Arabic writing, most notably the writing of the <a href="/wiki/Glottal_stop" title="Glottal stop">glottal stop</a> or <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hamzah" title="Hamzah">hamzah</a></i> (which was preserved in the eastern dialects but lost in western speech) and the use of <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="ar ALA transliteration">alif maqṣūrah</span></i> (representing a sound preserved in the western dialects but merged with <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Arabic transliteration">ā</span></i> in eastern speech).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (November 2015)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Language_and_dialect">Language and dialect</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Language and dialect">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The sociolinguistic situation of Arabic in modern times provides a prime example of the linguistic phenomenon of <a href="/wiki/Diglossia" title="Diglossia">diglossia</a>, which is the normal use of two separate varieties of the same language, usually in different social situations. In the case of Arabic, educated Arabs of any nationality can be assumed to speak both their school-taught Standard Arabic as well as their native, mutually unintelligible "dialects";<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> these dialects linguistically constitute separate languages which may have dialects of their own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> When educated Arabs of different dialects engage in conversation (for example, a Moroccan speaking with a Lebanese), many speakers <a href="/wiki/Code-switching" title="Code-switching">code-switch</a> back and forth between the dialectal and standard varieties of the language, sometimes even within the same sentence. Arabic speakers often improve their familiarity with other dialects via music or film.</p>
<p>The issue of whether Arabic is one language or many languages is politically charged, in the same way it is for the <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Chinese" title="Varieties of Chinese">varieties of Chinese</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hindi_language" title="Hindi language">Hindi</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Urdu_language" title="Urdu language">Urdu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Serbian_language" title="Serbian language">Serbian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Croatian_language" title="Croatian language">Croatian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Scots_language" title="Scots language">Scots</a> and English, etc. In contrast to speakers of Hindi and Urdu who claim they cannot understand each other even when they can, speakers of the varieties of Arabic will claim they can all understand each other even when they cannot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> The issue of diglossia between spoken and written language is a significant complicating factor: A single written form, significantly different from any of the spoken varieties learned natively, unites a number of sometimes divergent spoken forms. For political reasons, Arabs mostly assert that they all speak a single language, despite significant issues of mutual incomprehensibility among differing spoken versions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<p>From a linguistic standpoint, it is often said that the various spoken varieties of Arabic differ among each other collectively about as much as the <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance languages</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> This is an apt comparison in a number of ways. The period of divergence from a single spoken form is similar—perhaps 1500 years for Arabic, 2000 years for the Romance languages. Also, while it is comprehensible to people from the <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb">Maghreb</a>, a linguistically innovative variety such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Moroccan_Arabic" title="Moroccan Arabic">Moroccan Arabic</a> is essentially incomprehensible to Arabs from the <a href="/wiki/Mashriq" title="Mashriq">Mashriq</a>, much as French is incomprehensible to Spanish or Italian speakers but relatively easily learned by them. This suggests that the spoken varieties may linguistically be considered separate languages.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence_of_Arabic_on_other_languages">Influence of Arabic on other languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Influence of Arabic on other languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_Arabic_loanwords_in_English" title="List of Arabic loanwords in English">List of Arabic loanwords in English</a></div>
<p>The influence of Arabic has been most important in Islamic countries, because it is the language of the Islamic sacred book, the Quran. Arabic is also an important source of vocabulary for languages such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baluchi_language" title="Baluchi language">Baluchi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bengali_language" title="Bengali language">Bengali</a>, <a href="/wiki/Berber_languages" title="Berber languages">Berber</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bosnian_language" title="Bosnian language">Bosnian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Catalan_language" title="Catalan language">Catalan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chechen_language" title="Chechen language">Chechen</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dagestani_language" title="Dagestani language">Dagestani</a>, <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English</a>, <a href="/wiki/French_language" title="French language">French</a>, <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gujarati_language" title="Gujarati language">Gujarati</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hausa_language" title="Hausa language">Hausa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hindustani_language" title="Hindustani language">Hindustani</a>, <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Indonesian_language" title="Indonesian language">Indonesian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kazakh_language" title="Kazakh language">Kazakh</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kurdish_language" title="Kurdish language">Kurdish</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kutchi_Language" title="Kutchi Language">Kutchi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malay_language" title="Malay language">Malay</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pashto_language" title="Pashto language">Pashto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persian_language" title="Persian language">Persian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Punjabi_language" title="Punjabi language">Punjabi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Rohingya_language" title="Rohingya language">Rohingya</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saraiki_language" title="Saraiki language">Saraiki</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sicilian_language" title="Sicilian language">Sicilian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sindhi_language" title="Sindhi language">Sindhi</a>, <a href="/wiki/Somali_language" title="Somali language">Somali</a>, <a href="/wiki/Spanish_language" title="Spanish language">Spanish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Swahili_language" title="Swahili language">Swahili</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tagalog_language" title="Tagalog language">Tagalog</a>, <a href="/wiki/Turkish_language" title="Turkish language">Turkish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Urdu" title="Urdu">Urdu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Uzbek_language" title="Uzbek language">Uzbek</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Wolof_language" title="Wolof language">Wolof</a>, as well as other languages in countries where these languages are spoken.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In addition, English has many Arabic loanwords, some directly, but most via other Mediterranean languages. Examples of such words include admiral, adobe, alchemy, alcohol, algebra, algorithm, alkaline, almanac, amber, arsenal, assassin, candy, carat, cipher, coffee, cotton, ghoul, hazard, jar, kismet, lemon, loofah, magazine, mattress, sherbet, sofa, sumac, tariff, and many other words.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Other languages such as Maltese<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kinubi" title="Kinubi">Kinubi</a> derive ultimately from Arabic, rather than merely borrowing vocabulary or grammatical rules.</p>
<p>Terms borrowed range from religious terminology (like Berber <i>taẓallit</i>, "prayer", from <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salat" title="Salat">salat</a></i> (<span lang="ar">صلاة</span> <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Arabic transliteration">ṣalāh</span></i>)), academic terms (like <a href="/wiki/Uyghur_language" title="Uyghur language">Uyghur</a> <i>mentiq</i>, "logic"), and economic items (like English <i>coffee</i>) to <a href="/wiki/Placeholder_name" title="Placeholder name">placeholders</a> (like Spanish <i>fulano</i>, "so-and-so"), everyday terms (like Hindustani <i>lekin</i>, "but", or Spanish <i>taza</i> and French <i>tasse</i>, meaning "cup"), and expressions (like Catalan <i>a betzef</i>, "galore, in quantity"). Most Berber varieties (such as <a href="/wiki/Kabyle_language" title="Kabyle language">Kabyle</a>), along with Swahili, borrow some numbers from Arabic. Most Islamic religious terms are direct borrowings from Arabic, such as <span lang="ar">صلاة</span> (<i>salat</i>), "prayer", and <span lang="ar">إمام</span> (<i>imam</i>), "prayer leader."</p>
<p>In languages not directly in contact with the Arab world, Arabic loanwords are often transferred indirectly via other languages rather than being transferred directly from Arabic. For example, most Arabic loanwords in Hindustani and Turkish entered through Persian though Persian is an <a href="/wiki/Indo-Iranian_languages" title="Indo-Iranian languages">Indo-Iranian language</a>. Older Arabic loanwords in Hausa were borrowed from <a href="/wiki/Kanuri_language" title="Kanuri language">Kanuri</a>.</p>
<p>Some words in English and other European languages are derived from Arabic, often through other European languages, especially Spanish and Italian. Among them are commonly used words like "<a href="/wiki/Coffee" title="Coffee">coffee</a>" (<span lang="ar">قهوة</span> <i>qahwah</i>), "<a href="/wiki/Cotton" title="Cotton">cotton</a>" (<span lang="ar">قطن</span> <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Arabic transliteration">quṭn</span></i>), and "magazine" (<span lang="ar">مخازن</span> <i><a href="/wiki/Makhzen" title="Makhzen"><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="ar ALA transliteration">makhāzin</span></a></i>). English words more recognizably of Arabic origin include "<a href="/wiki/Algebra" title="Algebra">algebra</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Alcohol" title="Alcohol">alcohol</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Alchemy" title="Alchemy">alchemy</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Alkali" title="Alkali">alkali</a>", "<a href="/wiki/Zenith" title="Zenith">zenith</a>", and "<a href="/wiki/Nadir" title="Nadir">nadir</a>".</p>
<p>Arabic words also made their way into several West African languages as Islam spread across the Sahara. Variants of Arabic words such as <span lang="ar">كتاب</span> <i>kitāb</i> ("book") have spread to the languages of African groups who had no direct contact with Arab traders.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>Since throughout the Islamic world, Arabic occupied a position similar to that of Latin in Europe, many of the Arabic concepts in the fields of science, philosophy, commerce, etc. were coined from Arabic roots by non-native Arabic speakers, notably by Aramaic and Persian translators, and then found their way into other languages. This process of using Arabic roots, especially in Kurdish and Persian, to translate foreign concepts continued through to the 18th and 19th centuries, when swaths of Arab-inhabited lands were under <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman</a> rule.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence_of_other_languages_on_Arabic">Influence of other languages on Arabic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Influence of other languages on Arabic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The most important sources of borrowings into (pre-Islamic) Arabic are from the related (Semitic) languages <a href="/wiki/Aramaic_language" title="Aramaic language">Aramaic</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> which used to be the principal, international language of communication throughout the ancient Near and Middle East, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ge%27ez_language" title="Ge'ez language">Ethiopic</a>, and to a lesser degree Hebrew (mainly religious concepts). In addition, many cultural, religious and political terms have entered Arabic from <a href="/wiki/Iranian_languages" title="Iranian languages">Iranian languages</a>, notably <a href="/wiki/Middle_Persian" title="Middle Persian">Middle Persian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Parthian_language" title="Parthian language">Parthian</a>, and (Classical) Persian,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup> and Hellenistic Greek (<i>kīmiyāʼ</i> has as origin the Greek <i>khymia</i>, meaning in that language the melting of metals; see <a href="/wiki/Roger_Dachez" title="Roger Dachez">Roger Dachez</a>, <i>Histoire de la Médecine de l'Antiquité au XXe siècle</i>, Tallandier, 2008, p. 251), <i>alembic</i> (distiller) from <i>ambix</i> (cup), <i>almanac</i> (climate) from <i>almenichiakon</i> (calendar). (For the origin of the last three borrowed words, see Alfred-Louis de Prémare, <i>Foundations of Islam</i>, Seuil, L'Univers Historique, 2002.) Some Arabic borrowings from Semitic or Persian languages are, as presented in De Prémare's above-cited book:</p>
<ul>
<li><i>madīnah</i>/<a href="/wiki/Medina" title="Medina">medina</a> (مدينة, city or city square), a word of Aramaic or Hebrew origin מדינה (in which it means "a state");</li>
<li><i>jazīrah</i> (جزيرة), as in the well-known form الجزيرة "Al-Jazeera," means "island" and has its origin in the Syriac ܓܙܝܪܗ <i>gazīra</i>.</li>
<li><i>lāzaward</i> (لازورد) is taken from Persian لاژورد <i>lājvard</i>, the name of a blue stone, lapis lazuli. This word was borrowed in several European languages to mean (light) blue - azure in English, <i>azur</i> in French and <i>azul</i> in Portuguese and Spanish.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Arabic_alphabet_and_nationalism">Arabic alphabet and nationalism</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Arabic alphabet and nationalism">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There have been many instances of national movements to convert Arabic script into Latin script or to Romanize the language. Currently, the only Arabic language to use Latin script is <a href="/wiki/Maltese_language" title="Maltese language">Maltese</a>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lebanon">Lebanon</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Lebanon">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Beirut newspaper <i>La Syrie</i> pushed for the change from Arabic script to Latin letters in 1922. The major head of this movement was <a href="/wiki/Louis_Massignon" title="Louis Massignon">Louis Massignon</a>, a French Orientalist, who brought his concern before the Arabic Language Academy in Damacus in 1928. Massignon's attempt at Romanization failed as the Academy and population viewed the proposal as an attempt from the Western world to take over their country. <a href="/wiki/Sa%27id_al-Afghani" title="Sa'id al-Afghani">Sa'id Afghani</a>, a member of the Academy, mentioned that the movement to Romanize the script was a <a href="/wiki/Zionism" title="Zionism">Zionist</a> plan to dominate Lebanon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shrivtiel179_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-shrivtiel179-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shirvtiel188_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-shirvtiel188-34">[34]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Egypt">Egypt</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Egypt">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>After the period of colonialism in Egypt, Egyptians were looking for a way to reclaim and re-emphasize Egyptian culture. As a result, some Egyptians pushed for an Egyptianization of the Arabic language in which the formal Arabic and the colloquial Arabic would be combined into one language and the Latin alphabet would be used.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shrivtiel179_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-shrivtiel179-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shirvtiel188_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-shirvtiel188-34">[34]</a></sup> There was also the idea of finding a way to use <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hieroglyphics" title="Hieroglyphics">Hieroglyphics</a> instead of the Latin alphabet, but this was seen as too complicated to use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shrivtiel179_33-2"><a href="#cite_note-shrivtiel179-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shirvtiel188_34-2"><a href="#cite_note-shirvtiel188-34">[34]</a></sup> A scholar, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salama_Musa" title="Salama Musa">Salama Musa</a> agreed with the idea of applying a Latin alphabet to Arabic, as he believed that would allow Egypt to have a closer relationship with the West. He also believed that Latin script was key to the success of Egypt as it would allow for more advances in science and technology. This change in alphabet, he believed, would solve the problems inherent with Arabic, such as a lack of written vowels and difficulties writing foreign words that made it difficult for non-native speakers to learn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shrivtiel179_33-3"><a href="#cite_note-shrivtiel179-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shirvtiel188_34-3"><a href="#cite_note-shirvtiel188-34">[34]</a></sup> Ahmad Lutfi As Sayid and Muhammad Azmi, two Egyptian intellectuals, agreed with Musa and supported the push for Romanization.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shrivtiel179_33-4"><a href="#cite_note-shrivtiel179-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shrivtiel189_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-shrivtiel189-35">[35]</a></sup> The idea that Romanization was necessary for modernization and growth in Egypt continued with Abd Al-Aziz Fahmi in 1944. He was the chairman for the Writing and Grammar Committee for the Arabic Language Academy of Cairo.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shrivtiel179_33-5"><a href="#cite_note-shrivtiel179-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shrivtiel189_35-1"><a href="#cite_note-shrivtiel189-35">[35]</a></sup> However, this effort failed as the Egyptian people felt a strong cultural tie to the Arabic alphabet.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shrivtiel179_33-6"><a href="#cite_note-shrivtiel179-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-shrivtiel189_35-2"><a href="#cite_note-shrivtiel189-35">[35]</a></sup> In particular, the older Egyptian generations believed that the Arabic alphabet had strong connections to Arab values and history, which is easy to believe due to the long history of the Arabic alphabet (Shrivtiel, 189) in Muslim societies.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="The_language_of_the_Quran_and_its_influence_on_Poetry">The language of the Quran and its influence on Poetry</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: The language of the Quran and its influence on Poetry">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a> introduced a new way of writing to the world. People began studying applying the unique styles they learned from the Quran into not only their own writing, but also their <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">culture</a>. The deep level on which the Quran addresses the reader creates a strong bond and connection to the reader's soul. Writers studied the unique <a href="/wiki/Structure" title="Structure">structure</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Text_formatting" title="Text formatting">format</a> of the Quran in order to identify and apply the figurative devices and their impact on the reader.</p>
<h3><span id="Quran's_figurative_devices"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Quran.27s_figurative_devices">Quran's figurative devices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Quran's figurative devices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The Quran inspired musicality in poetry through the internal rhythm of the verses. The arrangement of words, how certain sounds create harmony, and the agreement of rhymes create the sense of rhythm within each verse. At times, the chapters of the Quran only have the rhythm in common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nicholson_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nicholson-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>The repetition in the Quran introduced the true power and impact repetition can have in poetry. The repetition of certain words and phrases made them appear more firm and explicit in the Quran. The Quran uses constant metaphors of blindness and deafness to imply unbelief. Metaphors were not a new concept to poetry, however the strength of extended metaphors was. The explicit imagery in the Quran inspired many poets to include and focus on the feature in their own work. The poet <a href="/wiki/Abdallah_ibn_al-Mu%27tazz" title="Abdallah ibn al-Mu'tazz">ibn al mu'tazz</a> wrote a book regarding the figures of speech inspired by his study of the Quran. OPoets such as badr Shakir al sayyab expresses his political opinion in his work through imagery inspired by the forms of more harsher imagery used in the Quran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Allen-37">[37]</a></sup> The Quran uses figurative devices in order to express the meaning in the most beautiful form possible. The study of the pauses in the Quran as well as other rhetoric allow it to be approached in a multiple ways.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adonis_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Adonis-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Structure">Structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Although the Quran is known for its <a href="/wiki/Fluency" title="Fluency">fluency</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harmony" title="Harmony">harmony</a>, the structure can be best described as <a href="/wiki/Chaotic" title="Chaotic">chaotic</a>. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Suras" title="Suras">suras</a> also known as verses of the Quran are not placed in chronological order. The only constant in their structure is that the longest are placed first and shorter ones follow. The topics discussed in the chapter often have no relation to each other and only share their sense of <a href="/wiki/Rhyme" title="Rhyme">rhyme</a>. The Quran introduces to poetry the idea of abandoning order and scattering narratives throughout the text. Harmony is also present in the sound of the Quran. The elongations and accents present in the Quran create a harmonious flow within the writing. Unique sound of the Quran recited, due to the <a href="/wiki/Diacritic" title="Diacritic">accents</a>, create a deeper level of understanding through a deeper emotional connection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-Allen-37">[37]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Quran is written in a language that is simple and understandable by people. The simplicity of the writing inspired later poets to write in a more clear and clear-cut style.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen_37-2"><a href="#cite_note-Allen-37">[37]</a></sup> The words of the Quran, although unchanged, are to this day understandable and frequently used in both formal and informal Arabic. The simplicity of the language makes memorizing and reciting the Quran a slightly easier task.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Culture_and_the_Quran">Culture and the Quran</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Culture and the Quran">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The writer <a href="/wiki/I%27jaz" title="I'jaz">al-Khattabi</a> explains how <a href="/wiki/Culture" title="Culture">culture</a> is a required element to create a sense of art in work as well as understand it. He believes that <a href="/wiki/Fluency" title="Fluency">fluency</a> and <a href="/wiki/Harmony" title="Harmony">harmony</a> the Quran possess are not the only elements that make it beautiful and create a bond between the reader and the text. While a lot of poetry was deemed comparable to the Quran in that it is equal to or better than the composition of the Quran, a debate rose that such statements are not possible because humans are incapable of composing work comparable to the Quran.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Adonis_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Adonis-38">[38]</a></sup> Because the structure of the Quran made it difficult for a clear timeline to be seen, <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">Hadith</a> were the main source of chronological order. The Hadith were passed down from generation to generation and this tradition became a large resource for understanding the context. Poetry after the Quran began possessing this element of <a href="/wiki/Tradition" title="Tradition">tradition</a> by including ambiguity and background information to be required to understand the meaning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nicholson_36-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nicholson-36">[36]</a></sup></p>
<p>After the Quran came down to the people, the tradition of memorizing the <a href="/wiki/Verse_(poetry)" title="Verse (poetry)">verses</a> became present. It is believed that the larger amount of the Quran memorized is a sign of a stronger faith. As technology improved overtime, hearing <a href="/wiki/Recitation" title="Recitation">recitations</a> of Quran became more available as well as more tools to help memorize the versus. The tradition of Love Poetry served as a symbolic representation of a Muslim's desire for a closer contact with their Lord.</p>
<p>While the influence of the Quran on Arabic poetry is explained and defended by numerous writers, some writers such as <a href="/wiki/Al-Baqillani" title="Al-Baqillani">Al- Baqillani</a> believe that poetry and the Quran are in no conceivable way related due to the <a href="/wiki/Uniqueness" title="Uniqueness">uniqueness</a> of the Quran. Poetry's imperfections prove his points that that they cannot be compared with the fluency the Quran holds.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Arabic_and_Islam">Arabic and Islam</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Arabic and Islam">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Classical Arabic is the language of poetry and literature (including news); it is also mainly the language of the <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">Quran</a>. At present, Modern Standard Arabic (MSA) is also used in modernized versions of literary forms of the Quran. Arabic is closely associated with the religion of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a> because the Quran was written in Arabic, but it is nevertheless also spoken by other religious groups such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arab_Christians_and_Arabic-speaking_Christians" title="Arab Christians and Arabic-speaking Christians">Arab Christians</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mizrahi_Jews" title="Mizrahi Jews">Mizrahi Jews</a>, <a href="/wiki/Druze" title="Druze">Druze</a> and Iraqi <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mandaean" title="Mandaean">Mandaeans</a>. Most of the world's <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muslims" title="Muslims">Muslims</a> do not speak Classical Arabic as their native language, but many can read the Quranic script and recite the Quran. Among non-Arab Muslims, translations of the Quran are most often accompanied by the original text.</p>
<p>Some Muslims present a <a href="/wiki/Proto-Human_language" title="Proto-Human language">monogenesis</a> of languages and claim that the Arabic language was the language revealed by God for the benefit of mankind and the original language as a prototype system of symbolic communication, based upon its system of triconsonantal roots, spoken by man from which all other languages were derived, having first been corrupted.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-meforum_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-meforum-40">[40]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">Judaism</a> has a similar account with the <a href="/wiki/Tower_of_Babel" title="Tower of Babel">Tower of Babel</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dialects_and_descendants">Dialects and descendants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Dialects and descendants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Arabic" title="Varieties of Arabic">Varieties of Arabic</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Arabic_Dialects.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="950" data-file-width="1681" height="170" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Arabic_Dialects.svg/300px-Arabic_Dialects.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Arabic_Dialects.svg/450px-Arabic_Dialects.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Arabic_Dialects.svg/600px-Arabic_Dialects.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Arabic_Dialects.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Different dialects of Arabic.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><i>Colloquial Arabic</i> is a collective term for the spoken dialects of Arabic used throughout the <a href="/wiki/Arab_world" title="Arab world">Arab world</a>, which differ radically from the literary language. The main dialectal division is between the varieties within and outside of the Arabian peninsula, followed by that between <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Arabic#Sedentary_vs._Bedouin" title="Varieties of Arabic">sedentary</a> varieties and the much more conservative <a href="/wiki/Bedouin" title="Bedouin">Bedouin</a> varieties. All of the varieties outside of the Arabian peninsula (which include the large majority of speakers) have a large number of features in common with each other that are not found in Classical Arabic. This has led researchers to postulate the existence of a prestige koine dialect in the one or two centuries immediately following the Arab conquest, whose features eventually spread to all of the newly conquered areas. (These features are present to varying degrees inside the Arabian peninsula. Generally, the Arabian peninsula varieties have much more diversity than the non-peninsula varieties, but have been understudied.)</p>
<p>Within the non-peninsula varieties, the largest difference is between the non-Egyptian <a href="/wiki/Maghrebi_Arabic" title="Maghrebi Arabic">North African dialects</a> (especially Moroccan Arabic) and the others. Moroccan Arabic in particular is hardly comprehensible to Arabic speakers east of <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a> (although the converse is not true, in part due to the popularity of Egyptian films and other media).</p>
<p>One factor in the differentiation of the dialects is influence from the languages previously spoken in the areas, which have typically provided a significant number of new words and have sometimes also influenced pronunciation or word order; however, a much more significant factor for most dialects is, as among Romance languages, retention (or change of meaning) of different classical forms. Thus Iraqi <i>aku</i>, Levantine <i>fīh</i> and North African <i>kayən</i> all mean 'there is', and all come from Classical Arabic forms (<i>yakūn</i>, <i>fīhi</i>, <i>kā'in</i> respectively), but now sound very different.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Examples">Examples</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Examples">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Transcription is a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Broad_transcription" title="Broad transcription">broad IPA transcription</a>, so minor differences were ignored for easier comparison. Also, the <a href="/wiki/Arabic_phonology" title="Arabic phonology">pronunciation</a> of <a href="/wiki/Modern_Standard_Arabic" title="Modern Standard Arabic">Modern Standard Arabic</a> differs significantly from region to region.</p>

</td>
