<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Koine">Koine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Koine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to <a href="/wiki/Charles_A._Ferguson" title="Charles A. Ferguson">Charles A. Ferguson</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> the following are some of the characteristic features of the <a href="/wiki/Koin%C3%A9_language" title="Koiné language">koine</a> that underlies all of the modern dialects outside the Arabian peninsula. Although many other features are common to most or all of these varieties, Ferguson believes that these features in particular are unlikely to have evolved independently more than once or twice and together suggest the existence of the koine:</p>
<ul>
<li>Loss of the <a href="/wiki/Dual_(grammatical_number)" title="Dual (grammatical number)">dual (grammatical number)</a> except on nouns, with consistent plural agreement (cf. feminine singular agreement in plural inanimates).</li>
<li>Change of <i>a</i> to <i>i</i> in many affixes (e.g., non-past-tense prefixes <i>ti- yi- ni-</i>; <i>wi-</i> 'and'; <i>il-</i> 'the'; feminine <i>-it</i> in the <a href="/wiki/Construct_state" title="Construct state">construct state</a>).</li>
<li>Loss of third-weak verbs ending in <i>w</i> (which merge with verbs ending in <i>y</i>).</li>
<li>Reformation of geminate verbs, e.g., <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Arabic transliteration">ḥalaltu</span></i> 'I untied' → <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Arabic transliteration">ḥalēt(u)</span></i>.</li>
<li>Conversion of separate words <i>lī</i> 'to me', <i>laka</i> 'to you', etc. into indirect-object <a href="/wiki/Clitic" title="Clitic">clitic</a> suffixes.</li>
<li>Certain changes in the <a href="/wiki/Cardinal_number_(linguistics)" title="Cardinal number (linguistics)">cardinal number</a> system, e.g., <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Arabic transliteration">khamsat ayyām</span></i> 'five days' → <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Arabic transliteration">kham(a)s tiyyām</span></i>, where certain words have a special plural with prefixed <i>t</i>.</li>
<li>Loss of the feminine <a href="/wiki/Elative_(gradation)" title="Elative (gradation)">elative</a> (comparative).</li>
<li>Adjective plurals of the form <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Arabic transliteration">kibār</span></i> 'big' → <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Arabic transliteration">kubār</span></i>.</li>
<li>Change of <a href="/wiki/Arabic_grammar#Nisba" title="Arabic grammar">nisba</a> suffix <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Arabic transliteration">-iyy</span></i> &gt; <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Arabic transliteration">i</span></i>.</li>
<li>Certain lexical items, e.g., <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Arabic transliteration">jāb</span></i> 'bring' &lt; <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Arabic transliteration">jāʼa bi-</span></i> 'come with'; <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Arabic transliteration">shāf</span></i> 'see'; <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Arabic transliteration">ēsh</span></i> 'what' (or similar) &lt; <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Arabic transliteration">ayyu shayʼ</span></i> 'which thing'; <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Arabic transliteration">illi</span></i> (relative pronoun).</li>
<li>Merger of <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɮˤ/</span> and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ðˤ/</span>.</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Dialect_groups">Dialect groups</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Dialect groups">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Egyptian_Arabic" title="Egyptian Arabic">Egyptian Arabic</a> is spoken by around 53 million in <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> (55 million worldwide).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EA_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-EA-42">[42]</a></sup> It is one of the most understood varieties of Arabic, due in large part to the widespread distribution of Egyptian films and television shows throughout the Arabic-speaking world</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Levantine_Arabic" title="Levantine Arabic">Levantine Arabic</a> includes <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/North_Levantine_Arabic" title="North Levantine Arabic">North Levantine Arabic</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/South_Levantine_Arabic" title="South Levantine Arabic">South Levantine Arabic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cypriot_Arabic" title="Cypriot Arabic">Cypriot Arabic</a>. It is spoken by about 21 million people in <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Jordan</a>, <a href="/wiki/State_of_Palestine" title="State of Palestine">Palestine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Israel" title="Israel">Israel</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Turkey" title="Turkey">Turkey</a>.
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Lebanese_Arabic" title="Lebanese Arabic">Lebanese Arabic</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Arabic" title="Varieties of Arabic">variety</a> of <a href="/wiki/Levantine_Arabic" title="Levantine Arabic">Levantine Arabic</a> spoken primarily in <a href="/wiki/Lebanon" title="Lebanon">Lebanon</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Jordanian_Arabic" title="Jordanian Arabic">Jordanian Arabic</a> is a continuum of mutually intelligible varieties of <a href="/wiki/Levantine_Arabic" title="Levantine Arabic">Levantine Arabic</a> spoken by the population of the <a href="/wiki/Jordan" title="Jordan">Kingdom of Jordan</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Palestinian_Arabic" title="Palestinian Arabic">Palestinian Arabic</a> is a name of several dialects of the subgroup of <a href="/wiki/Levantine_Arabic" title="Levantine Arabic">Levantine Arabic</a> spoken by the <a href="/wiki/Palestinians" title="Palestinians">Palestinians</a> in <a href="/wiki/Palestinian_National_Authority" title="Palestinian National Authority">Palestine</a>, by <a href="/wiki/Arab_citizens_of_Israel" title="Arab citizens of Israel">Arab citizens of Israel</a> and in most Palestinian populations around the world.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Samaritan_alphabet" title="Samaritan alphabet">Samaritan Arabic</a>, spoken by only several hundred in the <a href="/wiki/Nablus" title="Nablus">Nablus</a> region</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cypriot_Maronite_Arabic" title="Cypriot Maronite Arabic">Cypriot Maronite Arabic</a>, spoken in <a href="/wiki/Cyprus" title="Cyprus">Cyprus</a></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Maghrebi_Arabic" title="Maghrebi Arabic">Maghrebi Arabic</a>, also called "Darija" spoken by about 70 million people in <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a>, <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a>, Libya and Malta. It is very hard to understand for Arabic speakers from the Mashriq or Mesopotamia, the easiest being Libyan Arabic and the hardest Moroccan Arabic and Maltese language (which is close to Tunisian Arabic). The others such as Algerian Arabic can be considered "in between".
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Libyan_Arabic" title="Libyan Arabic">Libyan Arabic</a> spoken in <a href="/wiki/Libya" title="Libya">Libya</a> and neighboring countries.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Tunisian_Arabic" title="Tunisian Arabic">Tunisian Arabic</a> spoken in <a href="/wiki/Tunisia" title="Tunisia">Tunisia</a> and North-eastern <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Algerian_Arabic" title="Algerian Arabic">Algerian Arabic</a> spoken in <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Judeo-Arabic" title="Judeo-Arabic">Judeo-Algerian Arabic</a> was spoken by <a href="/wiki/History_of_the_Jews_in_Algeria" title="History of the Jews in Algeria">Jews</a> in <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a> until 1962</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Moroccan_Darija" title="Moroccan Darija">Moroccan Arabic</a> spoken in <a href="/wiki/Morocco" title="Morocco">Morocco</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Maltese_language" title="Maltese language">Maltese</a>, spoken on the island of Malta, is the only dialect to have established itself as a fully separate language,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> with independent literary norms. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sicilian_Arabic" title="Sicilian Arabic">Sicilian Arabic</a>, spoken on the island of <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a> until the 14th century, developed into Maltese in Malta. In the course of its history the language has adopted numerous loanwords, phonetic and phonological features, and even some grammatical patterns, from Italian, Sicilian and English. It is also the only Semitic language written in the <a href="/wiki/Latin_script" title="Latin script">Latin script</a>. Furthermore, Maltese or Sicilian Arabic are closely related to Tunisian Arabic due to the cultural and historical ties between Tunisia and Malta,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> and the languages are partially mutually intelligible.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Andalusian_Arabic" title="Andalusian Arabic">Andalusian Arabic</a>, spoken in <a href="/wiki/Spain" title="Spain">Spain</a> until the 16th century.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Siculo-Arabic" title="Siculo-Arabic">Siculo-Arabic</a>, was spoken in <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a> and <a href="/wiki/Malta" title="Malta">Malta</a> between the end of the ninth century and the end of the twelfth century.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mesopotamian_Arabic" title="Mesopotamian Arabic">Mesopotamian Arabic</a>, spoken by about 32 million people in Iraq (where it is called "Aamiyah"), eastern Syria and southwestern <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iran</a> (<a href="/wiki/Khuzestan_Province" title="Khuzestan Province">Khuzestan</a>).
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Baghdad_Arabic" title="Baghdad Arabic">Baghdad Arabic</a> is the Arabic dialect spoken in <a href="/wiki/Baghdad" title="Baghdad">Baghdad</a>, the capital of Iraq. It is a subvariety of <a href="/wiki/Mesopotamian_Arabic" title="Mesopotamian Arabic">Mesopotamian Arabic</a>.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kuwaiti_Arabic" title="Kuwaiti Arabic">Kuwaiti Arabic</a> is a <a href="/wiki/Gulf_Arabic" title="Gulf Arabic">Gulf Arabic</a> <a href="/wiki/Dialect" title="Dialect">dialect</a> spoken in <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Khuzestani_Arabic" title="Khuzestani Arabic">Khuzestani Arabic</a> spoken in the <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iranian</a> province of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Khuzestan" title="Khuzestan">Khuzestan</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Khorasani_Arabic" title="Khorasani Arabic">Khorasani Arabic</a> spoken in the <a href="/wiki/Iran" title="Iran">Iranian</a> province of <a href="/wiki/Khorasan_Province" title="Khorasan Province">Khorasan</a>.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sudanese_Arabic" title="Sudanese Arabic">Sudanese Arabic</a> is spoken by 17 million people in <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a> and some parts of southern Egypt. Sudanese Arabic is quite distinct from the dialect of its neighbor to the north; rather, the Sudanese have a dialect similar to the Hijazi dialect.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Juba_Arabic" title="Juba Arabic">Juba Arabic</a> spoken in <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a> and southern <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gulf_Arabic" title="Gulf Arabic">Gulf Arabic</a>, spoken by around four million people, predominantly in <a href="/wiki/Kuwait" title="Kuwait">Kuwait</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bahrain" title="Bahrain">Bahrain</a>, some parts of <a href="/wiki/Oman" title="Oman">Oman</a>, eastern Saudi Arabia coastal areas and some parts of <a href="/wiki/United_Arab_Emirates" title="United Arab Emirates">UAE</a> and Qatar. Also spoken in Iran's <a href="/wiki/Bushehr_Province" title="Bushehr Province">Bushehr</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hormozgan_Province" title="Hormozgan Province">Hormozgan</a> provinces. Although Gulf Arabic is spoken in Qatar, most Qatari citizens speak Najdi Arabic (Bedawi).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Yemeni_Arabic" title="Yemeni Arabic">Yemeni Arabic</a> spoken in <a href="/wiki/Yemen" title="Yemen">Yemen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Somalia" title="Somalia">Somalia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Djibouti" title="Djibouti">Djibouti</a> and southern Saudi Arabia by 15 million people. Similar to Gulf Arabic.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Najdi_Arabic" title="Najdi Arabic">Najdi Arabic</a>, spoken by around 10 million people, mainly spoken in Najd, central and northern Saudi Arabia. Most Qatari citizens speak Najdi Arabic (Bedawi).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hejazi_Arabic" title="Hejazi Arabic">Hejazi Arabic</a> (6 million speakers), spoken in Hijaz, western Saudi Arabia</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Hassaniya_Arabic" title="Hassaniya Arabic">Hassaniya Arabic</a> (3 million speakers), spoken in <a href="/wiki/Mauritania" title="Mauritania">Mauritania</a>, <a href="/wiki/Western_Sahara" title="Western Sahara">Western Sahara</a>, some parts of northern <a href="/wiki/Mali" title="Mali">Mali</a>, southern Morocco and south-western Algeria.</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Saharan_Arabic" title="Saharan Arabic">Saharan Arabic</a> spoken in some parts of <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">Niger</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mali" title="Mali">Mali</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Bahrani_Arabic" title="Bahrani Arabic">Bahrani Arabic</a> (600,000 speakers), spoken by <a href="/wiki/Bahrani_people" title="Bahrani people">Bahrani Shiʻah</a> in Bahrain and <a href="/wiki/Qatif" title="Qatif">Qatif</a>, the dialect exhibits many big differences from Gulf Arabic. It is also spoken to a lesser extent in Oman.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Judeo-Arabic_languages" title="Judeo-Arabic languages">Judeo-Arabic</a> dialects - these are the dialects spoken by the Jews that had lived or continue to live in the Arab World. As Jewish migration to Israel took hold, the language did not thrive and is now considered endangered.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Chadian_Arabic" title="Chadian Arabic">Chadian Arabic</a>, spoken in <a href="/wiki/Chad" title="Chad">Chad</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sudan" title="Sudan">Sudan</a>, some parts of <a href="/wiki/South_Sudan" title="South Sudan">South Sudan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_African_Republic" title="Central African Republic">Central African Republic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Niger" title="Niger">Niger</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nigeria" title="Nigeria">Nigeria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Cameroon" title="Cameroon">Cameroon</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Central_Asian_Arabic" title="Central Asian Arabic">Central Asian Arabic</a>, spoken in <a href="/wiki/Uzbekistan" title="Uzbekistan">Uzbekistan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tajikistan" title="Tajikistan">Tajikistan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Afghanistan" title="Afghanistan">Afghanistan</a>, is highly endangered</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Shirvani_Arabic" title="Shirvani Arabic">Shirvani Arabic</a>, spoken in <a href="/wiki/Azerbaijan" title="Azerbaijan">Azerbaijan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dagestan" title="Dagestan">Dagestan</a> until the 1930s, now extinct.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Phonology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Arabic_phonology" title="Arabic phonology">Arabic phonology</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="History_2">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Of the 29 Proto-Semitic consonants, only one has been lost: <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">*/ʒ/</span>,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="This sound is not given in the article on Proto-Semitic. Is it from an alternative formulation of the PS phonological system? (April 2015)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> which merged with <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ʃ/</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> But the consonant <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">*/ʒ/</span> is still found in many colloquial Arabic dialects. Various other consonants have changed their sound too, but have remained distinct. An original <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">*/p/</span> lenited to <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/f/</span>, and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">*/ɡ/</span> - consistently attested in pre-Islamic Greek transcription of Arabic languages<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> - became palatalized to <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɡʲ/</span> or <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɟ/</span> by the time of the Quran and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiced_palato-alveolar_affricate" title="Voiced palato-alveolar affricate">d͡ʒ</a>/</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiced_velar_stop" title="Voiced velar stop">ɡ</a>/</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiced_palato-alveolar_sibilant" title="Voiced palato-alveolar sibilant">ʒ</a>/</span> or <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɟ/</span> in MSA (see <a href="/wiki/Arabic_phonology#Local_variations" title="Arabic phonology">Arabic phonology#Local variations</a> for more detail).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup> An original <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_lateral_fricative" title="Voiceless alveolar lateral fricative">voiceless alveolar lateral fricative</a> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">*/ɬ/</span> became <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ʃ/</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harvcoltxt.7CWatson.7C2002.7Cp.3D2_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harvcoltxt.7CWatson.7C2002.7Cp.3D2-48">[48]</a></sup> Its <a href="/wiki/Emphatic_consonant" title="Emphatic consonant">emphatic</a> counterpart <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɬˠ~ɮˤ/</span> was considered by Arabs to be the most unusual sound in Arabic (Hence the Classical Arabic's appellation <span lang="ar">لُغَةُ ٱلضَّادِ</span> <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="ar ALA transliteration">lughat al-ḍād</span></i> or "language of the <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="ar ALA transliteration">ḍād</span></i>"); for most modern dialects, it has become an emphatic stop <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/dˤ/</span> with loss of the laterality<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harvcoltxt.7CWatson.7C2002.7Cp.3D2_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Harvcoltxt.7CWatson.7C2002.7Cp.3D2-48">[48]</a></sup> or with complete loss of any pharyngealization or velarization, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/d/</span>. (The classical <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="ar ALA transliteration">ḍād</span></i> pronunciation of <a href="/wiki/Pharyngealization" title="Pharyngealization">pharyngealization</a> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɮˤ/</span> still occurs in the <a href="/wiki/Mehri_language" title="Mehri language">Mehri language</a> and the similar sound without velarization, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiced_alveolar_lateral_fricative" title="Voiced alveolar lateral fricative">ɮ</a>/</span>, exists in other <a href="/wiki/Modern_South_Arabian_languages" title="Modern South Arabian languages">Modern South Arabian languages</a>.)</p>
<p>Other changes may also have happened. Classical Arabic pronunciation is not thoroughly recorded and different <a href="/wiki/Comparative_method_(linguistics)" title="Comparative method (linguistics)">reconstructions</a> of the sound system of Proto-Semitic propose different phonetic values. One example is the emphatic consonants, which are pharyngealized in modern pronunciations but may have been velarized in the eighth century and glottalized in Proto-Semitic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harvcoltxt.7CWatson.7C2002.7Cp.3D2_48-2"><a href="#cite_note-Harvcoltxt.7CWatson.7C2002.7Cp.3D2-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>Reduction of <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/j/</span> and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/w/</span> between vowels occurs in a number of circumstances and is responsible for much of the complexity of third-weak ("defective") verbs. Early Akkadian transcriptions of Arabic names shows that this reduction had not yet occurred as of the early part of the 1st millennium BC.</p>
<p>The Classical Arabic language as recorded was a poetic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Koine" title="Koine">koine</a> that reflected a consciously archaizing dialect, chosen based on the tribes of the western part of the <a href="/wiki/Arabian_Peninsula" title="Arabian Peninsula">Arabian Peninsula</a>, who spoke the most conservative variants of Arabic. Even at the time of Muhammed and before, other dialects existed with many more changes, including the loss of most glottal stops, the loss of case endings, the reduction of the diphthongs <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/aj/</span> and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/aw/</span> into monophthongs <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/eː, oː/</span>, etc. Most of these changes are present in most or all modern varieties of Arabic.</p>
<p>An interesting feature of the writing system of the Quran (and hence of Classical Arabic) is that it contains certain features of Muhammad's native dialect of Mecca, corrected through diacritics into the forms of standard Classical Arabic. Among these features visible under the corrections are the loss of the glottal stop and a differing development of the reduction of certain final sequences containing <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/j/</span>: Evidently, final <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/-awa/</span> became <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/aː/</span> as in the Classical language, but final <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/-aja/</span> became a different sound, possibly <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/eː/</span> (rather than again <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/aː/</span> in the Classical language). This is the apparent source of the <i>alif maqṣūrah</i> 'restricted alif' where a final <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/-aja/</span> is reconstructed: a letter that would normally indicate <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/j/</span> or some similar high-vowel sound, but is taken in this context to be a logical variant of <i>alif</i> and represent the sound <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/aː/</span>.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literary_Arabic">Literary Arabic</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Literary Arabic">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The "colloquial" spoken varieties of Arabic are learned at home and constitute the native languages of Arabic speakers. "Formal" <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Literary_Arabic" title="Literary Arabic">Literary Arabic</a> (usually specifically Modern Standard Arabic) is learned at school; although many speakers have a native-like command of the language, it is technically not the native language of any speakers. Both varieties can be both written and spoken, although the colloquial varieties are rarely written down and the formal variety is spoken mostly in formal circumstances, e.g., in radio broadcasts, formal lectures, parliamentary discussions and to some extent between speakers of different colloquial varieties. Even when the literary language is spoken, however, it is normally only spoken in its pure form when reading a prepared text out loud. When speaking <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Extemporaneous" title="Extemporaneous">extemporaneously</a> (i.e. making up the language on the spot, as in a normal discussion among people), speakers tend to deviate somewhat from the strict literary language in the direction of the colloquial varieties. In fact, there is a continuous range of "in-between" spoken varieties: from nearly pure Modern Standard Arabic (MSA), to a form that still uses MSA grammar and vocabulary but with significant colloquial influence, to a form of the colloquial language that imports a number of words and grammatical constructions in MSA, to a form that is close to pure colloquial but with the "rough edges" (the most noticeably "vulgar" or non-Classical aspects) smoothed out, to pure colloquial. The particular variant (or <i><a href="/wiki/Register_(sociolinguistics)" title="Register (sociolinguistics)">register</a></i>) used depends on the social class and education level of the speakers involved and the level of formality of the speech situation. Often it will vary within a single encounter, e.g., moving from nearly pure MSA to a more mixed language in the process of a radio interview, as the interviewee becomes more comfortable with the interviewer. This type of variation is characteristic of the <a href="/wiki/Diglossia" title="Diglossia">diglossia</a> that exists throughout the Arabic-speaking world.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;">
<div class="mediaContainer" style="width:180px"><audio class="kskin" controls="" data-durationhint="43.906598639456" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Poem_by_Abu_'ala_al-Ma'arri_(&quot;I_no_longer_steal_from_nature&quot;)_read_in_Arabic.ogg" data-startoffset="0" id="mwe_player_0" preload="none" style="width:180px">
<source data-bandwidth="131127" data-height="0" data-shorttitle="Ogg source" data-title="Original Ogg file (131 kbps)" data-width="0" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/2/28/Poem_by_Abu_%27ala_al-Ma%27arri_%28%22I_no_longer_steal_from_nature%22%29_read_in_Arabic.ogg" type='audio/ogg; codecs="vorbis"'/></audio></div>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Poem_by_Abu_%27ala_al-Ma%27arri_(%22I_no_longer_steal_from_nature%22)_read_in_Arabic.ogg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Recording of a poem by <a href="/wiki/Al-Ma%CA%BFarri#Asceticism" title="Al-Maʿarri">Al-Ma'arri titled "I no longer steal from nature"</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Although Modern Standard Arabic (MSA) is a unitary language, its pronunciation varies somewhat from country to country and from region to region within a country. The variation in individual "accents" of MSA speakers tends to mirror corresponding variations in the colloquial speech of the speakers in question, but with the distinguishing characteristics moderated somewhat. Note that it is important in descriptions of "Arabic" phonology to distinguish between pronunciation of a given colloquial (spoken) dialect and the pronunciation of MSA by these same speakers. Although they are related, they are not the same. For example, the phoneme that derives from Proto-Semitic <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/g/</span> has many different pronunciations in the modern spoken varieties, e.g., <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[d͡ʒ ~ ʒ ~ j ~ ɡʲ ~ ɡ]</span>. Speakers whose native variety has either <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiced_palato-alveolar_affricate" title="Voiced palato-alveolar affricate">d͡ʒ</a>]</span> or <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiced_palato-alveolar_sibilant" title="Voiced palato-alveolar sibilant">ʒ</a>]</span> will use the same pronunciation when speaking MSA. Even speakers from <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a>, whose native Egyptian Arabic has <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiced_velar_stop" title="Voiced velar stop">ɡ</a>]</span>, normally use <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiced_velar_stop" title="Voiced velar stop">ɡ</a>]</span> when speaking MSA. The <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Palatal_approximant#Palatal" title="Palatal approximant">j</a>]</span> of Persian Gulf speakers is the only variant pronunciation which isn't found in MSA; <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[d͡ʒ~ʒ]</span> is used instead.</p>
<p>Another example: Many colloquial varieties are known for a type of <a href="/wiki/Vowel_harmony" title="Vowel harmony">vowel harmony</a> in which the presence of an "emphatic consonant" triggers backed <a href="/wiki/Allophone" title="Allophone">allophones</a> of nearby vowels (especially of the low vowels <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/aː/</span>, which are backed to <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Open_back_unrounded_vowel" title="Open back unrounded vowel">ɑ(ː)</a>]</span> in these circumstances and very often fronted to <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Near-open_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Near-open front unrounded vowel">æ(ː)</a>]</span> in all other circumstances). In many spoken varieties, the backed or "emphatic" vowel allophones spread a fair distance in both directions from the triggering consonant; in some varieties (most notably Egyptian Arabic), the "emphatic" allophones spread throughout the entire word, usually including prefixes and suffixes, even at a distance of several syllables from the triggering consonant. Speakers of colloquial varieties with this vowel harmony tend to introduce it into their MSA pronunciation as well, but usually with a lesser degree of spreading than in the colloquial varieties. (For example, speakers of colloquial varieties with extremely long-distance harmony may allow a moderate, but not extreme, amount of spreading of the harmonic allophones in their MSA speech, while speakers of colloquial varieties with moderate-distance harmony may only harmonize immediately adjacent vowels in MSA.)</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Vowels">Vowels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Arabic&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Vowels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Modern Standard Arabic has six pure <a href="/wiki/Vowel" title="Vowel">vowels</a>, with short <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/a i u/</span> and corresponding long vowels <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/aː iː uː/</span>. There are also two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Diphthongs" title="Diphthongs">diphthongs</a>: <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/aj/</span> and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/aw/</span>.</p>
<p>The pronunciation of the vowels differs from speaker to speaker, in a way that tends to reflect the pronunciation of the corresponding colloquial variety. Nonetheless, there are some common trends. Most noticeable is the differing pronunciation of <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/a/</span> and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/aː/</span>, which tend towards fronted <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Near-open_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Near-open front unrounded vowel">æ(ː)</a>]</span>, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Open_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Open front unrounded vowel">a(ː)</a>]</span> or <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Open-mid_front_unrounded_vowel" title="Open-mid front unrounded vowel">ɛ(ː)</a>]</span> in most situations, but a back <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Open_back_unrounded_vowel" title="Open back unrounded vowel">ɑ(ː)</a>]</span> in the neighborhood of <a href="/wiki/Emphatic_consonant" title="Emphatic consonant">emphatic consonants</a>. Some accents and dialects, such as those of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hijaz" title="Hijaz">Hijaz</a>, have central <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Open_central_unrounded_vowel" title="Open central unrounded vowel">ä(ː)</a>]</span> in all situations. The vowel <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/a/</span> varies towards <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Mid_central_vowel#Mid_central_unrounded_vowel" title="Mid central vowel">ə(ː)</a>]</span> too. Listen to the final vowel in the recording of <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Arabic transliteration">al-ʻarabiyyah</span></i> at the beginning of this article, for example. The point is, Arabic has only three short vowel phonemes, so those phonemes can have a very wide range of allophones. The vowels <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/u/</span> and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɪ/</span> are often affected somewhat in emphatic neighborhoods as well, with generally more back or centralized <a href="/wiki/Allophone" title="Allophone">allophones</a>, but the differences are less great than for the low vowels. The pronunciation of short <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/u/</span> and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/i/</span> tends towards <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ʊ~o]</span> and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[i~e~ɨ]</span>, respectively, in many dialects.</p>
<p>The definition of both "emphatic" and "neighborhood" vary in ways that reflect (to some extent) corresponding variations in the spoken dialects. Generally, the consonants triggering "emphatic" allophones are the <a href="/wiki/Pharyngealization" title="Pharyngealization">pharyngealized</a> consonants <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/tˤ dˤ sˤ ðˤ/</span>; <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_uvular_stop" title="Voiceless uvular stop">q</a>/</span>; and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_trill" title="Alveolar trill">r</a>/</span>, if not followed immediately by <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/i(ː)/</span>. Frequently, the <a href="/wiki/Velar_consonant" title="Velar consonant">velar</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fricative" title="Fricative">fricatives</a> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/x ɣ/</span> also trigger emphatic allophones; occasionally also the <a href="/wiki/Pharyngeal_consonant" title="Pharyngeal consonant">pharyngeal consonants</a> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ʕ ħ/</span> (the former more than the latter). Many dialects have multiple emphatic allophones of each vowel, depending on the particular nearby consonants. In most MSA accents, emphatic coloring of vowels is limited to vowels immediately adjacent to a triggering consonant, although in some it spreads a bit farther: e.g., <span lang="ar">وقت</span> <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Arabic transliteration">waqt</span></i> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[wɑqt]</span> 'time'; <span lang="ar">وطن</span> <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Arabic transliteration">waṭan</span></i> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[wɑtˤɑn]</span> 'homeland'; <span lang="ar">وسط المدينة</span> <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Arabic transliteration">wasṭ al-madīnah</span></i> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[wæstˤɑl-mædiːnɐ]</span> 'downtown' (sometimes <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[wɑstˤɑl-mædiːnæ]</span> or similar).</p>
<p>In a non-emphatic environment, the vowel <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/a/</span> in the diphthong <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/aj/</span> tends to be fronted even more than elsewhere, often pronounced <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[æj]</span> or <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɛj]</span>: hence <span lang="ar">سيف</span> <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Arabic transliteration">sayf</span></i> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[sajf ~ sæjf ~ sɛjf]</span> 'sword' but <span lang="ar">صيف</span> <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Arabic transliteration">ṣayf</span></i> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[sˤɑjf]</span> 'summer'. However, in accents with no emphatic allophones of <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/a/</span> (e.g., in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hijaz" title="Hijaz">Hijaz</a>), the pronunciation <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[äj]</span> occurs in all situations.</p>

<p>The phoneme <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/d͡ʒ/</span> is represented by the Arabic letter <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="ar ALA transliteration">jīm</span></i> (<span lang="ar"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%D8%AC" title="ج">ج</a></span>) and has many standard pronunciations. <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiced_palato-alveolar_affricate" title="Voiced palato-alveolar affricate">d͡ʒ</a>]</span> is characteristic of north Algeria, Iraq, also in most of the Arabian peninsula but with an allophonic <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiced_palato-alveolar_sibilant" title="Voiced palato-alveolar sibilant">ʒ</a>]</span> in some positions; <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiced_palato-alveolar_sibilant" title="Voiced palato-alveolar sibilant">ʒ</a>]</span> occurs in most of the <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a> and most North Africa; and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiced_velar_stop" title="Voiced velar stop">ɡ</a>]</span> is used in most of Egypt and some regions in Yemen and Oman. Generally this corresponds with the pronunciation in the colloquial dialects.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson_2002_16_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-Watson_2002_16-49">[49]</a></sup> In some regions in Sudan and Yemen, as well as in some Sudanese and Yemeni dialects, it may be either <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɡʲ]</span> or <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiced_palatal_stop#Palatal_or_alveolo-palatal" title="Voiced palatal stop">ɟ</a>]</span>, representing the original pronunciation of Classical Arabic. Foreign words containing <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiced_velar_stop" title="Voiced velar stop">ɡ</a>/</span> may be transcribed with <span dir="rtl" lang="ar"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%D8%AC" title="ج">ج</a></span>‎, <span dir="rtl" lang="ar"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%D8%BA" title="غ">غ</a></span>‎, <span dir="rtl" lang="ar"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%D9%83" title="ك">ك</a></span>‎, <span dir="rtl" lang="ar"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%D9%82" title="ق">ق</a></span>‎, <span dir="rtl" lang="fa"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%DA%AF" title="گ">گ</a></span>‎, <span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-family:Harmattan,Scheherazade,Lateef,LateefGR,Alkalami,Amiri,'DejaVu Sans',FreeSerif,'Noto Naskh Arabic','Droid Arabic Naskh','Arabic Typesetting','Sakkal Majalla','Traditional Arabic','Simplified Arabic','Caribri','Segoe UI','Microsoft Sans Serif',Arial,'Times New Roman',serif,sans-serif; font-size: 125%; font-weight:normal;"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%DD%A3" title="ݣ">ݣ</a>‎</span> or <span class="script-arabic script-Arab" dir="rtl" style="font-family:Harmattan,Scheherazade,Lateef,LateefGR,Alkalami,Amiri,'DejaVu Sans',FreeSerif,'Noto Naskh Arabic','Droid Arabic Naskh','Arabic Typesetting','Sakkal Majalla','Traditional Arabic','Simplified Arabic','Caribri','Segoe UI','Microsoft Sans Serif',Arial,'Times New Roman',serif,sans-serif; font-size: 125%; font-weight:normal;">ڨ‎</span>, mainly depending on the regional spoken variety of Arabic or the commonly diacriticized Arabic letter. Note also that in northern Egypt, where the Arabic letter <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Arabic transliteration">jīm</span></i> (<span lang="ar"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%D8%AC" title="ج">ج</a></span>) is normally pronounced <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiced_velar_stop" title="Voiced velar stop">ɡ</a>]</span>, a separate phoneme <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/<a href="/wiki/Voiced_palato-alveolar_sibilant" title="Voiced palato-alveolar sibilant">ʒ</a>/</span>, which may be transcribed with <span lang="ar">چ</span>, occurs in a small number of mostly non-Arabic loanwords, e.g., <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ʒakitta/</span> 'jacket'.</p>
<p><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/θ/</span> (<span lang="ar">ث</span>) can be pronounced as <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_dental_and_alveolar_stops#Alveolar" title="Voiceless dental and alveolar stops">t</a>]</span> or even <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_fricative#Voiceless_alveolar_sibilant" title="Voiceless alveolar fricative">s</a>]</span>. In some places of Maghreb it can be also pronounced as <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_sibilant_affricate" title="Voiceless alveolar sibilant affricate">t͡s</a>]</span>.</p>
<p><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/x/</span> and <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɣ/</span> (<span lang="ar">خ,‎ غ</span>) are velar, post-velar, or uvular.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Watson_2002_18_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Watson_2002_18-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>In many varieties, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ħ, ʕ/</span> (<span lang="ar">ح,‎ ع</span>) are actually <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epiglottal_consonant" title="Epiglottal consonant">epiglottal</a> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ʜ, ʢ]</span> (despite what is reported in many earlier works).</p>
<p><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/l/</span> is pronounced as velarized <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Alveolar_lateral_approximant#Velarized_alveolar_lateral_approximant" title="Alveolar lateral approximant">ɫ</a>]</span> in الله <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ʔallaːh/</span>, the name of God, q.e. <a href="/wiki/Allah" title="Allah">Allah</a>, when the word follows <i>a</i>, <i>ā</i>, <i>u</i> or <i>ū</i> (after <i>i</i> or <i>ī</i> it is unvelarized: <span lang="ar">بسم الله</span> <i>bismi l–lāh</i> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/bismillaːh/</span>). Some speakers velarize other occurrences of <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/l/</span> in MSA, in imitation of their spoken dialects.</p>
<p>The emphatic consonant <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/dˤ/</span> was actually pronounced <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɮˤ]</span>, or possibly <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[d͡ɮˤ]</span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup>—either way, a highly unusual sound. The medieval Arabs actually termed their language <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="ar ALA transliteration">lughat al-ḍād</span></i> 'the language of the <a href="/wiki/%E1%B8%8C%C4%81d" title="Ḍād">Ḍād</a>' (the name of the letter used for this sound), since they thought the sound was unique to their language. (In fact, it also exists in a few other minority Semitic languages, e.g., Mehri.)</p>
<p>Arabic has consonants traditionally termed "emphatic" <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/tˤ, dˤ, sˤ, ðˤ/</span> (<span lang="ar">ط,‎ ض,‎ ص,‎ ظ</span>), which exhibit simultaneous <a href="/wiki/Pharyngealization" title="Pharyngealization">pharyngealization</a> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[tˤ, dˤ, sˤ, ðˤ]</span> as well as varying degrees of <a href="/wiki/Velarization" title="Velarization">velarization</a> <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[tˠ, dˠ, sˠ, ðˠ]</span>, so they may be written with the "Velarized or pharyngealized" diacritic ( <big><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">̴</span></big> ) as: <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/t̴, d̴, s̴, ð̴/</span>. This simultaneous articulation is described as "Retracted Tongue Root" by phonologists.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> In some transcription systems, emphasis is shown by capitalizing the letter, for example, <span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/dˤ/</span> is written ⟨D⟩; in others the letter is underlined or has a dot below it, for example, ⟨<span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Arabic transliteration">ḍ</span>⟩.</p>
<p>Vowels and consonants can be phonologically short or long. Long (<a href="/wiki/Gemination" title="Gemination">geminate</a>) consonants are normally written doubled in Latin transcription (i.e. bb, dd, etc.), reflecting the presence of the <a href="/wiki/Arabic_diacritics" title="Arabic diacritics">Arabic diacritic</a> mark <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Arabic transliteration">shaddah</span></i>, which indicates doubled consonants. In actual pronunciation, doubled consonants are held twice as long as short consonants. This consonant lengthening is phonemically contrastive: <span lang="ar">قبل</span> <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Arabic transliteration">qabila</span></i> 'he accepted' vs. <span lang="ar">قبّل</span> <i><span class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none" title="Arabic transliteration">qabbala</span></i> 'he kissed'.</p>
